ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.         When the first significant agricultural development projects were
undertaken in Ethiopia in the mid-1960's, the main emphasis was on comprehen-
sive projects designed to develop limited areas through intensive application
of a number of inputs. Important among such projects have been the Chilalo
Agricultural Development Project (CADU) (established with assistance from the
Swedish International Development Agency) and the First Wolamo Agricultural
Development Project (WADU), assisted by IDA. The Wolamo project has been
successful in demonstrating the efficacy of improved agricultural inputs pro-
vided in conjunction with farmer credit, infrastructure, extension and other
services. The rate of farmer participation in the project has been far better
than was originally expected, and significiant yield increases resulted in
increasing cash incomes for a large segment of the rural population. The
experience gained from the comprehensive projects has more recently been
utilized in the Agricultural Minimum Package Project, which concentrates on
the country-wide distribution of farm inputs on credit to smallholders.
ii.        The proposed Project would continue and expand both in scope and area
the investments being carried out under the first Wolamo project. Although its
main component would be the expansion of crop production through the provision
of farm inputs, it would also introduce on a wide scale improvement of live-
stock through crossbreeding and animal health programs. This would be achieved
mainly through the expansion of WADU's extension, technical and administrative
services. Support would be provided for cooperatives and cooperative marketing
centers, which would provide an institutional basis for the distribution of
credit and the marketing of produce. A soil conservation program would be
established which - through limited land rehabilitation, contour ploughing,
gully stabilization and studies - would help provide the basis for the formu-
lation of a more extensive program of erosion control in Ethiopia. An experi-
mental coffee program would be carried out in an effort to increase crop qual-
ity, yields and production. In addition, the Project would also include pro-
vision for construction and upgrading of rural roads, expanded rural water
facilities, and assistance to rural artisans.
iii.       WADU - the project unit established under the first project - would
continue as executing authority for the proposed Project; however, the Project
envisages the increasing devolution of implementation responsibility to coop-
eratives and to regular Government agencies. The Project is part of a con-
tinuing program; its full benefits would, however, not be attained until the
end of the ninth year when, with continuation of some of the services provided
by WADU and AIDB, the Project would have benefitted about three quarters of
the Districts' 150,000 farmers. On completion, further development could be
undertaken either by means of another specific project, or as part of Govern-
ment's overall development program.



- ii -
iv.        An IDA credit of US$12.0 million is proposed which would cover the
foreign exchange component of the Project and 49% of local costs over the five
year period. This would represent 69% of total Project costs of Eth$35.9 mil-
lion (US$17.3 million), or 75% of Project costs net of taxes and duties. Gov-
ernment would onlend funds for seasonal credit to AIDB at 7-1/4% for a term
of 15 years. AIDB would onlend to WFCU at 10%, and it in turn would onlend to
the registered cooperatives at 11% per annum; these would then onlend to farmers
at 12% per annum. Credit for cooperative marketing centers (CMC's) would be
channelled to farmers through WADU at the above rates. Farmers' contribution
would consist of a 25% cash downpayment for farm inputs and a small cash con-
tribution for rural water facilities. Imported goods, mostly equipment, vehi-
cles, fertilizer and other farm inputs valued at US$3.5 million would be procured
by international competitive bidding in accordance with Bank/IDA guidelines.
Construction of WADU Headquarters would be subject to local competitive bidding.
All other civil works would be carried out by WADU force account. Contracts
for goods and services costing less than US$30,000 would follow Government pro-
curement procedures which are satisfactory; orders would be bulked whenever
possible.
v.         At full development about 80% of the net value of incremental pro-
duction would be attributable to crops - notably maize and teff - which would
progressively eliminate the District's dependence on imports from other parts
of Ethiopia.  The Project would also support crop diversification through the
production of cotton, chillies, potatoes and pulses. Total incremental produc-
tion of maize would be 38,000 tons in year 5, rising to 58,000 tons at full
development.  Incremental production of teff would likewise rise from 8,000
tons in year 5 to 13,000 tons in year 12. The Project would also result in
increased livestock output, thus providing expanded quantities of meat and
dairy products.
vi.        The Project's economic rate of return would be 17% over twenty
years. The major risk facing the Project would be an increase in costs;
cost increases of 10% or 20% would reduce the return to 14% and 11% respec-
tively, while a 10% cost increase along with a 10% benefit reduction would
give a return of 10%. Rates of return were calculated separately for the
crop, livestock, soil conservation and coffee programs; each of these in-
d-tviduai returns would be satisfactory. The Project would increase the
cashi incomes of about 73,000 participating farmers:  for owner operators
ULcremental incomes would range from Eth$30 to Eth$144, and for tenants
from Eth$20 to Eth$135 annually.
vii.       This would be the Bank Group's ninth lending for agricultural
development in Ethiopia. Eight IDA assisted projects have been approved: six
iri agriculture - the First Wolamo, Humera, Amibara Irrigation, Coffee Pro-
cessing, and the Minimum Package Projects; two for livestock development -
the Addia Ababa Dairy and the Second Livestock Development Project; and one
to support commercial farm credit - the Agricultural and Industrial Develop-
ment Bank (AIDB) Project. Performance under all these projects has generally
been satisfactory, although considerable delays have been experienced in the
start-up of a number of projects.
viii. The Project is suitable for an IDA Credit of US$12.0 million to
the Empire of Ethiopia.



ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The first significant agricultural development projects were under-
taken in Ethiopia in the mid-1960's and the main emphasis was on comprehensive
projects designed to develop limited areas through intensive application of
a number of inputs. Important among such projects have been the Chilalo
Agricultural Development Project (CADU) - established with assistance from the
Swedish International Development Agency - and the First Wolamo Agricultural
Development Project (WADU) - assisted by IDA. The experience and technical
expertise gained from these projects have more recently been utilized under
Government's Minimum Package Program, also supported by IDA.
1.02      The First Wolamo Agricultural Development Project concentrated on
two sub-districts within Wolamo District. The project has been successful
in demonstrating the efficacy of improved agricultural inputs provided in
conjunction with farmer credit, infrastructure, extension and other services.
The rate of farmer participation in the project was far better than was orig-
inally expected, and significant yield increases resulted in greater cash
incomes for a large segment of the rural population. The proposed Project
would intensify and expand these services throughout the District, with par-
ticular emphasis on the introduction of improved livestock production.
1.03      This would be the Bank Group's ninth lending for agricultural devel-
opment in Ethiopia. Eight IDA assisted projects have been approved: six in
agriculture - the First Wolamo, Humera, Amibara Irrigation, Coffee Processing,
and the Minimum Package Projects; two for livestock development - the Addis
Ababa Dairy and the Second Livestock Development Project; and one to support
commercial farm credit - the Agricultural and Industrial Development Bank
(AIDB) Project. Performance under all these projects has generally been
satisfactory, although considerable delays have been experienced in the start-
up of a number of projects.
1.04      The proposed Project was prepared by the Ministry of Agriculture
with assistance of the Bank's Resident Mission in Eastern Africa. This report
is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited Ethiopia in
October/November 1973 comprising Messrs. E. Zimmer-Vorhaus, T. Baddar, H.
Malik (IDA) and M. Nightingale (consultant).



- 2 -
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.01      Ethiopia, with an area of about 1.2 million km2 lies in the north-
eastern zone of Africa just north of the Equator. Much of the country is
high plateau 1,500 to 3,000 meters above sea level and dissected by gorges
and broad valleys. Although there is no comprehensive land use data, it is
estimated that over 50% of the nation's land area is used as permanent pasture;
forests and crops each account for an additional 9%. The population of about
25 million is growing at a rate of 2.4% per year. Over 90% of the people
live in the rural areas - some 85% in the highlands - but the urban popula-
tion is estimated to be growing at about 7% per year. Gross Domestic Product
(GDP), estimated at US$2.1 billion in 1971/1972, is growing at about 4.5% per
year, while per capita income is estimated at about US$80 per year.
B. The Agricultural Sector
Main Features
2.02      The agricultural sector is distinguished by a wide climatic and
ecological diversity which allows a large variety of crops to be grown. The
peasant farmers possess a high level of traditional agricultural skills, but
they lack an efficient marketing system, access roads, security of tenure,
credit, extension and veterinary services and improved seeds, as well as
fertilizer and other farming inputs. Thus the peasant sector, which is
largely untouched by modern technology, is characterized by low human and
resource productivity. Although eight of every ten Ethiopians are peasant
farmers, agriculture contributed only about 52% of GDP in 1971/72. Average
total annual family output (at farm prices) is less than Eth$300 (US$140),
and 75% of this represents subsistence consumption. The total value of agri-
cultural output in 1970 was about Eth$1,900 million; cereals, coffee and
other crops accounted for 71% and livestock and livestock products for the
remaining 29%.
2.03      Agricultural products have regularly made up about 90% of total
exports; coffee contributes about 60%, and other major contributors are oil-
seeds, pulses, hides and skins, and animals, meat, fruit and vegetables.
Imports of agricultural products were valued at about Eth$33 million in 1972;
50% of these consisted of cotton and cereals, while the remaining 50% comprised
manufactured food items, notably tea and tobacco. Actual growth of agricul-
tural output during the Third Five Year Development Plan (1968/69 - 1973/74)
was estimated at about 2% a year, less than the rate of population increase.
This may be explained in part by the fact that the planned investment targets
for agriculture were not able to be met due, among other things, to the drop



- 3 -
in coffee prices and the decline in the inflow of private capital.  TLe recent
drought which has been experienced in some provinces will affect crop pro-
duction for many years, as a lack of seeds and work oxen which cannot be
replaced in one year will lead to a reduction in the total cultivated area.
The drought has not significantly affected Wolamo District, however.
The Livestock Sector-
2.04      Ethiopia's livestock includes some 26 million cattle and 12 mil-
lion each of sheep and goats. It is estimated that in 1972 livestock and
livestock products with a total export value of about Eth$60 million
contributed about 29% of total agricultural output. The recent drought has
caused heavy losses of livestock in some provinces and this is expected to
result in an overall decrease in livestock production over the next few
years.
2.05      There are many constraints to livestock development; insecure land
tenure arrangements deprive the farmer of incentives to control stocking, to
plant fodder crops, improve pasture, or develop stock watering facilities.
Diseases are a further constraint; it is estimated that 1.2 million cattle
and 2 million sheep and goats (mostly young stock) are lost to disease annually.
Uncontrolled grazing and stock breeding have also denuded vast areas of range-
lands. The cattle are traditionally viewed as a supply of work-oxen rather
than as a source of money income, and this attitude would have to be consider-
ably changed before modern livestock management could have a major impact among
peasant farmers.
C. Government Services
2.06      Up to 1971 the extension services of the Ministry of Agriculture
were limited and there were only about 100 extension staff available. In
1971 the Extension and Project Implementation Denartment (EPID) was created
to provide technical and administrative supervision of agricultural devel-
opment projects. EPID has considerable administrative autonomy in personnel
and fiscal management, and in 1973 employed over 250 extension staff. In
addition to carrying out limited extension activities outside ongoing project
areas, EPID acts as the agent of the Agricultural and Industrial Development
Bank (AIDB) in areas where no cooperatives exist; it thus distributes farm
inputs, handles credit accounts and collects repayment from farmers.
2.07      The Veterinary Services Department of the Ministry of Agriculture,
staffed by about 50 veterinarians located throughout the country, is respon-
sible for disease control, meat inspection and quarantine services; it also
provides diagnostic and clinical facilities to farmers and is responsible for
animal health education. The Livestock and Meat Board (LMB), a parastatal
agency created in 1964, is charged with improving the livestock economy and
with promotion of livestock production and sales.



- 4 -
2.08      There  is no national homogeneous grain market in Ethiopia, as
production areas are widely separated and inadequate transport facilities make
the movement of grain from one location to another difficult. Other marketing
problems include the absence of accurate market or price information outside
the network of traders and merchants, failure to enforce standard weights and
measures and a shortage of capital to carry and store grains. A project to
help alleviate these difficulties is now under preparation.
Credit and Supply of_Inputs
2.09      In the past there has been little institutional lending to small
farmers, partly because of lack of cooperatives or farmers groups, and partly
because of inadequate institutions at the national level. Farmers generally
obtain credit from merchants, wealthier farmers or relatives and interest
rates on this borrowing can be as high as 10X a month or more. AIDB was
established in 1970 to meet the financial requirements of agricultural, in-
dustrial and commercial enterprises by providing long, medium and short-term
loans and equity investments in commercially viable development schemes.
Agricultural Inputs and Marketing Services (AIMS) was established as a sub-
sidiary of AIDB to procure and import agricultural inputs for distribution
to cooperatives, farmers and agricultural development projects. An IDA
Credit of US$11.0 million was provided in 1972 to help AIDB expand its op-
erations in industry, commerce and agriculture. The Credit included funds
for an agricultural credit survey to be carried out by consultants; this
study is now nearing completion.
D. Cooperative Development
2.10      In 1966 the Cooperative Development Department was established in
the Ministry of National Community Development and Social Affairs (MNCD).
This Department is responsible for the promotion, registration, audit, in-
spection and supervision of cooperatives as well as assisting them in the
marketing of agricultural produce. The Department has 55 professional staff,
and operates a training center for staff and cooperative members. By June
1972, 116 cooneratives had been formed under its supervision. The 67 multi-
purpose agricultural cooperative societies established have a total member-
ship of 15,000 persons and a share capital of Eth$1.5 million; forty of these
have been registered and 27 are in the process of formation. Societies provide
marketing, credit and supply services; in June, 1972 registered societies had
an annual turnover of Eth$7.7 million and outstanding loans amounted to Eth$9.8
million.
III. FIRST WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
3.01      The First Wolamo Agricultural Development Project (1970) was de-
signed to be the first six-year phase of an integrated intensive program to
provide farm inputs, infrastructure and extension services in densely popu-
lated highland areas, and to establish new settlements in less habited savanna



areas. The project was located in Wolamo District (Awraja) of Sidamo Province,
which has an area of about 3,180 km2 and a population of about 730,000. Al-
though not significantly affected by the recent drought, the district is
typical of the backward conditions prevalent throughout the highland areas.
Development was concentrated in two of Wolamo's seven subdistricts and the
Project was implemented by the Wolamo Agricultural Development Unit (WADU),
established as special unit within the Ministry of Agriculture. The IDA
Credit of US$3.5 million covered about 69% of total project costs of US$5.1
million.
3.02      The first project aimed at improved crop production for about
6,000 farmers in the highland areas of the District and the settlement of
some 1,750 families on 6 ha farms in two new or reorganized settlements.
This was to be achieved through the provision of land planning and settlement
advice; road construction, land clearing and soil conservation; water supplies;
extension services including a farmer training center and a livestock inno-
culation campaign; construction of marketing and coffee processing centers;
and agricultural credit for improved seed, fertilizer, and farm implements.
These services were designed to increase production of maize, wheat, teff,
seed cotton and chillies, to improve the quality of coffee produced in the
region, and to improve the health of livestock herds. In order to facilitate
distribution of inputs and credit, and to provide a mechanism for credit'col-
lection and the marketing of produce, five marketing centers (four in the
highlands and one in settlements) were included under the project. Primary
cooperative societies were to be set up at each market, and it was envisaged
that at a later stage a cooperative union would be formed to take over re-
sponsibility for the centralized services provided by WADU.
3.03      The project was slow in getting started, mainly due to delays in
staff recruitment and coordination problems with local authorities. However,
implementation has since progressed well and project funds are now expected
to be fully committed by mid-1974, about 18 months ahead of schedule. The
early completion date can be attributed mainly to a significantly higher rate
of farmer participation over that expected at appraisal, resulting in higher
per year expenditures (about 20%) on farm inputs and related extension and
marketing services. To a limited extent this increase has been offset by
lower than expected expenditures on some services - notably the provision
of rural water supplies.
3.04      The most significant achievement of the project has been that more
farmers than expected at appraisal participated in the extension program,
which concentrated on the introduction of improved inputs and included advice
on animal husbandry and home economics. Thirteen extension areas (as opposed
to the five originally projected) have been established, each organized around
a marketing center. Associated with each marketing center is a cooperative,
which farmers interested in receiving credit joined after payment of a nominal
registration fee; in 1973/74 about 7,200 farmers had received short term credit
totalling about Eth$400,000 through these centers. Interest on loans was set
at 12%, and repayment of credit has been excellent, averaging about 97% over
the first three years.



-6-
3.05      As the first project is still in operation, data on the last project
year (1973/74) achievements is still tentative. However, best estimates are
that by Year 4 (1973/74), incremental production under the project would equal
that originally forecast for Year 5. The total value of incremental production
of all project crops (maize, cotton, coffee, chillies, teff,wheat and sorghum)
is now estimated at Eth$1.6 million in Year 4, as compared with an appraisal
estimate of Eth$1.0 million.   Yields have been significantly higher than
expected, due mainly to the enthusiastic response of farmers to the improved
farm inputs. For example, actual yields of maize were already beyond those
forecast (2,000 kg/ha as opposed to 1,300 kg/ha), as were those of wheat
(1,700 kg/ha as opposed to 1,200 kg/ha) and cotton (1,000 kg/ha as compared
with 560 kg/ha).
3.06      The land tenure situation in Ethiopia is unsatisfactory and pre-
sents a severe constraint on any far-reaching attempt to modernize the
agricultural sector. The position in Wolamo is no exception: while the
average holding is about 1.5 ha, tenants - who constitute about half of the
farming population - cultivate an average of only about 0.5 ha and make rent
payments to their landlords both in kind and cash plus some labor contribu-
tion. Although livestock plays an important role in the farming system,
there is virtually no common grazing land and the highly fragmentized system
of farm production tends to discourage farmers from making any significant
changes in their current practices. Despite these fundamental difficulties,
progress under the first project has shown that owner operators and tenants
can gain important benefits from a soundly based comprehensive development
program. Accordingly, while land reform must be regarded as central to any
fundamental improvement to the rural life of Ethiopia, expected benefits
under a second project in Wolamo would not be dependent on changes in the
present land tenure system and expected improvements in tennants' incomes
would be sufficient to ensure their participation.
3.07      While physical progress in establishing the settlement schemes pro-
"ided under the first project has been satisfactory, Government has been slow
to legalize settlers rights of tenure. Accordingly it would be a condition
of Credit effectiveness that Government would have issued leases to these
settlers on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA.
3.08      USAID furnished the project's total third and fourth season require-
ment for fertilizer, thus releasing for other purposes the revolving fund of
some Eth$280,000 which had been accumulated through farmers' repayment of
input credit. Under the proposed Project these funds would be retained and
used at WADU's discretion to meet the ongoing working capital requirements of
the cooperatives. Assurances were obtained that WADU would provide IDA with
audited statements of the revolving fund at the end of each financial period.
3.09      The first project has demonstrated the effectiveness of a broadly
based rural development program, and the willingness of farmers to respond
to the provision of credit where it is accompanied by extension advice and
other supporting services. Thus far, however, the project has reached only
a small proportion of the District's population. The proposed Project would
seek to extend existing and new services throughout the District.



-7-
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
4.01      The Project would over five years continue and expand the rural
development program initiated under the First Wolamo Development Project to
increase the productivity and incomes of small farmers. It would provide
credit for incremental agricultural inputs, support for cooperative develop-
ment, facilities for livestock development, and technical and administrative
services to established small farmers. 1/ While WADU would continue as
executing authority, the Project envisages the increasing devolution of imple-
mentation responsibility to cooperatives and to regular Government agencies.
4.02      The specific Project components and their relationship to services
in first project areas would be:
(1) In first project areas (two of W4olamo's seven sub-districts) the
Project would provide:
(a) Extension services to farmers not participating under
the first project and incremental agricultural inputs
and related credit, animal health, soil conservation
and home economic services for all farmers whether
participating or not;
(b) The introduction of new services, including livestock
cross-breeding, promotion of improved fodder crops, and
the establishment of a primary livestock market and
holding ground.
(2) Throughout the remaining areas (five sub-districts) not covered by
the first project, the Project would include: the introduction of
extension, elementary animal health, soil conservation and home
economics services, together with credit for the purchase of
agricultural inputs.
(3) In both first and second Project areas the Project would provide:
(a) Continued development and operational support for 13
cooperative marketing centers (CMC's) - four of which
have become registered cooperatives - and the Wolamo
Farmers Cooperative Union established under the first
project;
1/   The first project included provision for the settlement of 1,750 farmers;
however, the proposed Project would not include a settlement component as
land available for further settlement has not yet been identified. Pro-
vision would be made under the Project for a study to meet this need
(para. 4.21).



- 8 -
(b) Establishment of a further 39 CMC's, construction of about
600 km of minor roads and tracks (including upgrading of
tracks built under the first project), improvement of rural
water supplies, assistance to rural artisans, and the pro-
vision of expanded administrative services.
4.03      The Project is part of a continuing program extending the devel-
opments initiated under the first project. Its full benefits would, however,
not be attained until the end of the ninth year when, with continuation of
some of the services provided by WADU and AIDB, the Project would have
directly benefitted in varying degrees 100,000 farmers - about three quarters
of the Districts' farmers. On completion, further development could be under-
taken either by means of another specific project or as part of Government's
overall development program, according to what may be appropriate at that
time.
B. Detailed Features
Farm Inputs (Annex 1)
4.04      Project farmers would receive seasonal credit at an interest rate
of 1% per month for up to 11 months for the purchase of fertilizer (about 85%
of total inputs), improved seed, pesticides, tools and implements. In con-
formity with national policy, farmers would contribute 25% of the input cost
in the form of a cash downpayment. It is expected that 73,000 farmers (50%
of all District farmers) would have received credit by year 5, and about
100,000 (70% of all District farmers) would have been reached by year 9.
Cooperative Development (Annex 2)
4.05      The Project would establish an additional 39 Cooperative Marketing
Centers (CMC's) which would be responsible for the distribution of farm in-
puts and credit, repayment collection, and marketing of produce. Until regis-
tration as independent, self regulating cooperatives - expected about three
years after establishment - CMC's would be owned and operated by WADU, which
wvould pay the full cost of their staff. After registration this subsidy would
be gradually phased out to 75% of staff costs in year 4, 50% in year 5, 25%
in year 6, and zero by year 7. The Project would provide CMC's with simple
stores, offices and equipment towards which farmers would contribute - as was
successfully done under the first project - 50% of construction costs by
providing voluntary labor and some materials; the balance would be met by
WADU which would lease buildings and equipment to the CMC's free of charge.
Upon registration as a cooperative they would receive credit to acquire from
WADU buildings and equipment on terms and conditions approved by IDA.
Assurances to that effect were obtained. All registered cooperatives would
join the existing Wolamo Farmers Cooperative Union (WFCU) which would pro-
vide accounting, marketing and other support services and which WIADU would



- 9 -
join as the representative of the CMC's (para 4.13). Project funds for
credit for farmers enrolled in registered cooperatives would be channelled
directly from AIDB to WFCU, which in turn would onlend the funds to the cooper-
atives. Funds for CMC's would be channelled through WADU.
Rural Roads (Annex 3)
4.06      The Project would provide for the construction of 366 km of minor
roads and 235 km of tracks, including the upgrading of tracks constructed
under the first project to minor road standard. Minor roads would be pass-
able by 4-wheel drive vehicles during the rainy season, and would cost (exclud-
ing capital costs of equipment) an estimated Eth$4,200 per km; dry weather
tracks would be cleared and levelled at an estimated cost of Eth$1,200 per km.
Pending completion of a Government study to determine which agency should have
responsibility for rural road construction and maintenance throughout the
country, WkTADU would continue to be responsible for Project road construction.
The Project would provide additional road equipment to supplement that pro-
vided under the first project. As District revenues are now inadequate to
meet road maintenance costs, assurances were obtained that Government would
assume this specific responsibility.
Rural Water Supplies (Annex 4)
4.07      Rural water facilities in the Project area are inadequate, and plans
for the construction of piped water supplies in settlement areas as part of
the first project were not implemented due to the shortening of the project
period. The proposed Project would thus provide for pipelines and storage
tanks in settlement areas at an estimated cost of Eth$486,000, as well as
for the construction of wells, installation of hydrams, spring protection and
water storage in the highlands at a cost of about Eth$420,000. The cost of
construction and paintenance of these facilities would be recovered by water
charges in settlgaent areas. Elsewhere - in line with current practice - local
communities and the District administration would each contribute 25% of the
total costs; the balance would be covered by a WADU grant to the local com-
munities which would also maintain the facilities. Some 60,000 farm families
would benefit from these facilities. Assurances were obtained that the design
for the water supply system for the two settlements would be submitted to IDA
for comments before implementation.
Assistance to Rural Artisans
4.08      A variety of rural craft activities have emerged in Wolamo District
including cotton spinning and weaving, and the manufacture and repair of
farmer tools and equipment. However, up to this time no training has been
available for some 1,500 local artisans now involved. Because the needs have
not yet been clearly defined, WADU would prepare a small training program to
be included in the Project. The program is expected to include the training
and employment of instructors, and the extension of credit for the Purchase
of tools and equipment and spare parts for water supply facilities. Assur-
ances were obtained that 1WADU would submit this program to IDA for approval
before implementation.



- 10 -
WADU Services (Annex 5)
4.09      The Project would provide for WADU extension, technical and adminis-
trative services. Costs of these services (paras 4.10 - 4.19) were derived
from estimates of the total cost of the WADU program including the costs of
both the first and the proposed second projects; these costs were then allocated
on a pro rata basis between the two projects. Project costs thus include all
capital costs and about 85% of the recurrent costs of the program. Basic
services to farmers already participating under the first project would continue
under financing provided by Government outside the Project.
Extension and Training
4.10      Extension services provided under the Project would include demon-
strations with fertilizer and improved seed, organization of farmer groups
and advice on improved agricultural practices. To meet Project needs an
intensive farmer extension service would be required. Extension staff would
be built up over the Project period, and in year 5 would total about 200
persons including extension agents, senior demonstrators and demonstrators.
By Project completion the ratio of farmers to demonstrators throughout the
Project area would be about 500:1.
4.11      The Project would extend the soil conservation program established
under the first project (Annex 6). Demonstrators would supervise and train
farmer self-help groups working on contour plowing, gully stabilization and
land rehabilitation through tree planting; fourteen small tree nurseries would
be established to provide planting material at a nominal charge to farmers.
It is expected that 4,200 ha/year or a total of 21,000 ha would be protected
as part of this program.  Extension advice on home economics would also be
expanded; provision would be made for about 42 field workers, supporting
staff and equipment.
4+.12     The Project would also expand existing facilities at the Training
Center established under the first project. About 200 demonstrators and 600
rarmers would receive basic and advanced training over the Project period,
Marketing, Credit and Cooperative Services
4.13      The Project would through WlADU provide support services to CMC's,
registered cooperatives, and to the Wolamo Farmers Cooperative Union (WFCU)
knara 4.05).  These services would include help in processing credit applic-
atIons, collection of repayments, and ordering and distribution of farm in-
puts. WADU would advise WFCU and cooperatives on marketing procedures and
the collection of marketing data, and would provide financial, accounting
and other administrative services. It is expected that by year 9 WFCU would
assume full responsibility for these services. The Project would also pro-
vide for training of cooperative staff and assistance with registration.
These services would continue after the Project period either under WADU or
under the auspices of the Cooperative Department of the Ministry of National
Community Development.



- 1 1 -
Livestock Development (Annex 7)
4.14       The Project would extend the cattle crossbreeding and animal health
programs initiated under the first project. The crossbreeding program would
be concentrated in first project areas, and would eventually involve about
50,000 head of cattle (25% of the area herd) owned by about 7,000 farmers;
the remaining farmers with about 150,000 head would profit from the improved
standard of husbandry, and the fodder production and pasture improvement
programs carried out by the expanded livestock extension service. Fertilizer
and seeds for fodder crops, minerals and feed would be made available through
credit extended by the CMC's. In addition a small poultry up-grading program
would be introduced. To meet these needs the Project would include buildings,
machinery, equipment and staff for a Project farm which would produce both
Friesien bulls for the crossbreeding program and cocks for the poultry program.
4.15      A vaccination campaign and animal health services initiated under
the first project would be continued and expanded into the remainder of the
Project area with the addition of increased diagnostic support services and
extension staff. Credit would be made available through WADU for drugs,
although following Government policy, vaccinations themselves would be given
free of charge.
4.16      The Project would also establish a primary livestock market within
Wolamo District to facilitate the marketing of the Project's incremental an-
nual offtake - eventually reaching about 14,000 head. The market would be
owned and operated by the Livestock and Meat Board (LMB), which is respon-
sible for the livestock marketing program established under the IDA supported
Second Livestock Project. To serve this market WADU would set up and operate
a 1,000 ha holding ground.
Works and Services
4.17      The Project would provide continued support for WADU services,
including construction of a central headquarters building and water supply
to staff quarters and offices. The WADU workshop would be expanded to allow
maintenance and repair of the increased number of WADU vehicles and machines.
WADU Administration
4.18      WADU's administrative services would be expanded under the Project.
The existing division covering both Finance and Common Services would be se-
parated into two new divisions. The existing legal, audit and information
and public relations activities would receive additional support, as would
personnel, central services and purchasing sections.  Responsibility for
purchasing would be gradually taken over by EPID, whose Evaluation Unit would
also assist WADU's Planning, Evaluation and Budget Control Unit. Assurances
wdere obtained that WADU would adopt Project evaluation procedures acceptable
to IDA.



- 12 -
4.19      Under the Project the work of the crop trials section would be
expanded. Main emphasis would be on diversification of cash crops, fodder
crops and the response of both to fertilizers. Stores, offices and equipment
would be provided at six trial sites and at WADU headquarters. Project crop
trials are necessary to obtain data on local conditions, and would supplement
the work being carried out on a national scale by the Institute of Agricultural
Research (IAR). Hitherto there has been little coordination between these
programs, and therefore an assurance was obtained that an appropriate system
of research coordination would be set up between the Project and the IAR.
Experimental Program and Studies
4.20      Despite Ethiopia's heavy dependence on coffee both as a cash crop
and for its export earnings (para 2.03), production, yields and quality are
generally low. The first project provided some extension services aimed at
improving cultivation techniques, and under the Project these activities would
be developed through an experimental program which, if successful, could be
extended nationally. (Annex 8) Under this program extension advice would be
provided to about 1,000 participating farmers each year (5,000 over the Project
period). Demonstrators would stump one-seventh (about 25 bushes) of the coffee
bushes belonging to each farmer. As this would temporarily involve a loss of
revenue, farmers would be compensated by a cash subsidy of Eth$10 in the year
of cut-back, and fertilizer would be provided over five years free of charge
(equivalent to Eth$5 per farmer).
4.21      As part of the Project WADU, with the assistance of consultancy
advice where necessary, would also carry out three special studies:
(a) A monitoring program for the soil conservation program
(para 4.11) to provide data for the formulation of a more
extensive program of erosion control in Ethiopia;
(b) A study on the suitability of available land for further
settlement within the District; and
(c) A study of control and use of ranching land within the District.
Assurances were obtained that terms of reference and staffing arrangements
for these studies would be submitted to IDA for comment before commencement.
ECo10gY
4.22      There are not expected to be any adverse effects on the environment
as a result of the Project. The soil conservation program would help to
significantly reduce further erosion within the Project area.



- 13 -

of the program, the percentages being determined by the total number of farmers
benefitting from the first and second projects (para 4.09). Total Project
cost including taxes and duties is estimated at Eth$35.9 million (US$17.3
million), of which about US$6.8 million or 40% would represent foreign exchange.
Project costs are summarized below:

D. Financing
4.24      The financing of Project costs would be shared in the following
amounts and proportions:
US$'000         %
IDA                                                   12,000         75
Government                                             3,220         20
Farmers                                                  800          5
Project Cost Net of Taxes and Duties                  16,020        100
Taxes and Duties                                       1,300
Total Project Cost                                    17,320
The proposed IDA credit of US$12.0 million would be on standard terms to
Government.  This would finance the foreign exchange cost and about 49% of
local costs, or about 75% of total Project costs net of taxes and duties.
Government would onlend funds for seasonal credit for farm inputs (US$1.8
million) to AIDB at an interest rate of 7-1/4% for a term of 15 years with
5-year moratorium on principal. AIDB would onlend to WFCU at 10% per annum;
WFCU would in turn onlend to the registered cooperatives at 11% per annum,
and these would then onlend to farmers at 12% per annum. Credit for the CMC's
would be channelled to farmers through WADU at the above rates. Government's
contribution net of taxes and duties would be about Eth$6.6 million (US$3.2
million). Farmers contribution would represent the 25% cash downpayment for
farm inputs and a small cash contribution for rural water facilities. In
addition, farmers would also contribute unpaid labor to assist in the con-
struction of cooperative buildings and rural water facilities and in the soil
conservation program. All other Project funds would be channelled through
WADU. Government would continue to make available to WADU quarterly and in
advance the funds necessary to carry out WADU's annual work plan which WADU
would continue to submit in advance of each fiscal year for IDA approval.
Assurances as to the above terms and arrangements were obtained at nego-
tiations. The signing of a subsidiary loan agreement between Government and
AIDB would be a condition of Credit effectiveness. Government would bear
the foreign exchange risk.
E. Procurement
4.25      Equipment, vehicles, fertilizer and other farm inputs (US$3.5 mil-
lion) would be procured by international competitive bidding in accordance
with Bank/IDA guidelines. Construction of WADU headquarters (US$0.1 million)
is not likely to attract international bids and the contract would be awarded
to the lowest bidder on the basis of competitive bidding advertized locally.



- 15 -
All other civil works (US$1.4 million), which would be carried out in dif-
ferent locations and over a period of time, would be carried out by WADU
force account. Contracts for goods and services costing less than US$30,000
would follow Government procurement procedures which are acceptable; orders
would be grouped whenever possible. Assurances were obtained during nego-
tiations that the above procedures would be followed and that for all con-
tracts exceeding US$30,000, draft tender documents would be submitted for
IDA's approval before bid invitations, as would bid evaluations with recommen-
dations before award of contracts.
F. Disbursements (Annex 10)
4.26      Disbursements of funds from the Credit would be on following basis:
(a) 100% of CIF or 69% of total costs of vehicles, machinery
and equipment and incremental farm inputs including fertilizer,
improved seeds, and pesticides (US$2.7 million);
(b) 69% of total costs of civil works for roads including salaries,
construction material and operating costs of construction ma-
chinery (US$0.6 million); civil works for buildings (US$0.2
million); WADU's contribution to salaries of staff of marketing
centers and cooperatives, and to costs of cooperative buildings
and equipment (US$0.4 million);  studies on soil conservation,
availability of land for settlement, and use of ranching land
(US$0.2 million); assistance to rural artisans (US$0.2 million);
(c) 50% of total costs of the water supply for farmers and the two
settlements (US$0.2 million);
(d) 59% of total program costs of WADU salaries and operating expenses
(US$4.3 million);
(e) an unallocated amount of US$3.2 million representing contingencies
on the above categories and transferable to them as required.
Disbursement on all items would be made against import documents and appropriate
records of expenditure certified by the Project Director and the Finance Officer.
Any funds remaining on the Credit Account upon completion of the Project would
be used at the Association's discretion for the continuing development of the
District.
C. Accounts and Audit
4.27      The First Wolamo Agricultural Project had a high turnover of account-
ing staff and the simple accounting system was not properly maintained; farm-
ers' credit accounts, accounts of the revolving fund and the internal control



- 16 -
system were unsatisfactory, and the accounting system did not provide manage-
ment with prompt and informative accounts relating expenditures to budget.
Three new officers: the Finance Officer, the Internal Auditor and the Con-
troller of WADU's Marketing, Cooperative and Credit Division were recently
appointed to supplement WADU's existing staff, and a new accounting system
prepared by WADU's External Auditor is now being implemented. The Internal
Auditor is setting up new internal control procedures under which a budget
control officer would be added to WADU's Planning and Evaluation Section.
It is expected that with the addition of this new staff and the introduction
of improved accounting and budget control systems, WADU should be able to
produce accounts adequate to meet the needs of the Project.
4.28      Under the Project WADU's staff would be considerably expanded in
size and functions. Therefore, all CMC's although owned by WADU would be
treated as self-accounting units, as would the WFCU and the registered
cooperatives. Assurances to that effect were obtained at negotiations. As
the Project farm and the workshop would be revenue producing units, they would
adopt the accounting procedures necessary to adequately reflect the results
of their operations. Further assurances were obtained that: (i) WADU's
annual accounts would be audited by independent auditors acceptable to IDA,
and (ii) all CMC's and registered cooperatives would be audited semi-annually
by the Controller of the WFCU under WADU supervision, and the cooperatives
and WFCU would be audited annually by the Registrar of Cooperatives. Assur-
ances were obtained that these audited accounts would be submitted to IDA no
later than six months after expiration of the accounting periods.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
5.01     1WADU would have overall responsibility for Project implementation,
and would continue to operate as a self-contained unit of EPID. The inter-
ministerial committee which was responsible for coordination of the First
WoLamo Project activities has been superseded by a Ministerial Committee
chaired by the Minister of Agriculture; this committee was recently estab-
lished to develop general policies for and to oversee coordination of all
comPrehensive agricultural development projects in Ethiopia.  The Wolamo
District (Awraja) Development Committee chaired by the Wolamo Governor, es-
tablished under the first project, would be reconstituted on a broader basis
to link Project activities with other development in the District, and to
Lacilitate Project operations such as construction of roads and rural water
ftc.lities.  Members would include the Director of WADU, District repre-
sentatives of ministries, governors of sub-districts, farmer representatives
anid the chairman of the WFCU. Development committees would also be estab-
lished in each sub-district.
Project Administration
5.02      The Director of WADU would have direct responsibility for the
organization and administration of the Project. WADU would have six main
operating divisions (chart): Extension and Training (responsible for



- 17 -
agricultural extension activities, the coffee experimental program, land
planning and soil conservation); Marketing, Credit and Cooperatives; Live-
stock (responsible also for the Project farm); Works and Services (responsible
for roads, buildings, water supplies, repair and maintenance of vehicles and
equipment); Finance; and Common Services (responsible for personnel, central
services and minor purchases).  In addition, five units would report directly
to the Director of WADU: Planning, Evaluation and Budget Control; Information I
and Public Relations; Legal; Internal Audit; and Crop Trials.
5.03       The first project was successful in recruiting local staff to fill
a number of senior staff positions, including the Director of WADU. However,
to meet the expanded activities and the new services proposed under the Proj-
ect, it would be necessary to provide for the international recruitment of
seven staff for varying periods ranging from two to five years. These would
include: Deputy Director of WADU, Heads of the Extension and Training and
the Marketing and Credit Divisions, Heads of the Cooperative Development,
Artificial Insemination and Animal Health Sections, and Head of the Planning
and Evaluation Unit. Staff for three of those positions have previously been
recruited internationally under the first project. Assurances were obtained
that IDA would be consulted prior to appointments or reappointments to the
posts of Director of WADU, Heads of the Extension and Training, the Marketing
and Credit, and the Finance Divisions; and Heads of the Cooperative Development
and Artificial Insemination Sections.
Coordinating Agencies
5.04       EPID would provide assistance with Project monitoring and evaluation,
and in addition would gradually assume increased responsibility for all Project
procurement with the exception of farm inputs (which would remain the respon-
sibility of AIDB). The Wolamo Farmers Cooperative Union (WFCU) would onlend
and collect credit and would provide support services to all registered co-
operatives; these services would include distribution of inputs and marketing
of produce. The existing agreement between EPID/WADU and AIDB which sets the
terms and conditions of EPID/WADU's responsibility for input distribution and
extension and collection of credit as the agent of AIDB would be extended to
cover the Project period. The extension of this agreement and the conclusion
of a loan agreement between WFCU and AIDB on terms and conditions satisfactory
to IDA would be a condition of Credit effectiveness.
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING AND FARMERS BENEFITS
Production
6.01       At full development about 80% of the net value of the Project's
incremental production would be attributable to food crops, notably maize
and teff, which would progressively reduce and eliminate the District's
dependence on imports from other parts of Ethiopia (para 6.05). Using the



- 18 -
experience gained under the first phase, the Project would promote diver-
sification by the production of cotton, chillies, potatoes and pulses in
addition to the experimental coffee program. Yields, areas receiving Project
inputs and total incremental production are detailed below; except for an
extension of the total cultivated area by 20% (and a corresponding reduc-
tion of fallow land), total incremental production would be attributable to
increased yields.

In addition, the Project would result in limited production of lesser crops
including chillies (300 tons), irish potatoes (1,000 tons), haricot beans and
peas (600 tons).
6.02      Increased livestock production would result from three Project
comDonents: the crossbreeding and artificial insemination program, the
animal health and the livestock marketing components, and the intensified
extension service - which would also promote improved fodder production.
These Project components would result in total incremental annual sales of
9,800 head in year 5 and 14,000 head by year 12. The gross value of incre-
mental production would be about Eth$1 million in year 5, rising to Eth$2.3
milliorL in year 12.  In addition, by full development some incremental dairy
produce surplus to farm requirements would be available for sale in local
markets; this would be valued at Eth$1 million in year 12.
Marketing and Prices
6.03      There is at present no national grain market in Ethiopia 1/, and
although crop prices broadly reflect international commodity trends, they
are subject to wide regional and seasonal variations. As Wolamo is at present
a substantial net importer of food grains, it is expected that about 70% of
the Project's grain production would be consumed within the District and the
balance sold elsewhere in neighboring areas. Prices adopted (Annex 1, Table
5) are those based on recent first project experience; as compared with prices
1/   It is expected that a crop marketing and storage project now under
preparation will soon be submitted to IBRD for consideration.



- 19 -
assumed under the Minimum Package Project (mid-1972) - the latest IDA study
of Ethiopian grain prices - they show a price increase of about 60% for maize
(from Eth$8 to Eth$14 per quintal) but only a small increase for teff (from
Eth$19 to Eth$20 per quintal).
6.04      Ethiopia procures the fertilizer necessary to meet the requirements
of all projects by annual contract. Prices adopted for the Project are those
applying for the 1973/74 season; these show significant increases over farm
gate costs assumed under the Minimum Package Project (from Eth$38 to Eth$59
per quintal for DAP and from Eth$31 to Eth$50 for urea). While Project dif-
ferences (inputs, location, yields) do not enable a close comparison, for
maize cultivation the Project estimates a cost benefit ratio of 1:1.9 as
compared with 1:1.8 under Minimum Package. The ratio for teff shows a decline
from 1:3 to 1:1.8 which, while still offering reasonable inducement, is ex-
pected to result in a decline in farmer response; this expectation has been
reflected in Project planting programs. In the current uncertain conditions,
no price changes have been projected for inputs or crops; it is, however,
expected that any further increase in fertilizer costs would be reflected in
grain prices and that the cost benefit ratio of crop production would in the
light of recent experience remain broadly as indicated above. As of April
1974 the relation of international prices for grains and fertilizer had not
deteriorated. In 1973/74 Government introduced a small subsidy to temporarily
offset recent increases in fertilizer prices. As Government has not yet
decided whether to continue the subsidy, no account has been taken of it in
Proj ect calculations.
6.05       Cash crops produced under the Project (notably ginger, chillies,
pulses and cotton) would be sold at local markets and at present prices.
Quantities are not significant, and as some of present requirements have to
be imported into the District, no major price changes are anticipated. Milk
products (including ghee), poultry and meat are also expected to find ready
markets in the area (marketing of the latter would be assisted by a holding
ground and primary livestock market to be established under the Project)
(para 4.16).
Farmers Benefits (Annex 11)
6.06      Farmers estimated benefits have been based on five illustrative types
of farm enterprise that would be financed under the Project - each associated
with one of the five main ecological zones. The two zones covering invest-
ments under the first project, A and D (the latter representing settlement
areas), would mainly benefit from the livestock improvement program, par-
ticularly the crossbreeding program. Farmers in Zones B (constituting three-
quarters of all Project participants) and C would benefit from increased crop
production and elementary animal health services, while those in Zone E would
increase crop production and benefit from improved poultry production. Annex
11 shows that for owner operators (excluding settlements) the increase in
net incomes would range from Eth$42 to Eth$72 in Zone C and from Eth$235 to



- 20 -
Eth$380 in Zone A; tenants' incomes would increase from Eth$28 to Eth$48 in
Zone C and from Eth$181 to Eth316 in Zone A annually at full development.
The expected range of cost benefit ratios should be sufficient inducement
for farmers to use anticipated farm inputs. Tenants (who represent one half
of Wolamo farmers) pay rent partly in cash and partly in grains, but not in
livestock products; this should provide an incentive to their participation
in livestock development programs.
Government Budget and Foreign Exchange (Annex 12)
6.07      Government revenues from the Project would arise mainly from:  (a)
taxes on Project machinery, vehicles and equipment, and (b) the margin of
interest on the loan to AIDB. The Project would result in an increase in
recurrent Government expenditure (mainly for staff salaries) which would not
be matched by incremental revenues. The resulting annual deficit is projected
to be about Eth$1.5 million (US$0.7 million) up to year 15, when it would
increase to about Eth$2.0 million (US$1.0 million) per year. This compares
with a recurrent buget of Eth$17 million for all Government agricultural
services in 1973/74.
6.08      About 25% of Project annual incremental grain production of 50,000
tons by year 6 (mainly consisting of maize) is expected to offset imports of
grain into Ethiopia at a saving of about US$1 million. Project incremental
livestock production would not materially affect Government's foreign ex-
change position.
VII. ECONOMIC BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
7.01      The Project would intensify and expand the rural development
program initiated under the first project. It would at full development
(in year 9) substantially increase the cash incomes of about 100,000 par-
ticipating farmers, or about 70% of all farmers in Wolamo District. Through
the establishment of cooperatives, it would provide an institutional basis for
credit distribution and the marketing of produce. The livestock services
would improve the quality of local herds through crossbreeding and animal
health programs. The soil conservation program would through limited land
rehabilitation, contour ploughing, gully stabilization and studies, help pro-
vide the basis for the formulation of a more extensive program of erosion
control in Ethiopia. The provision of improved roads and rural water facil-
ities would benefit the entire District population; in particular, the roads
would facilitate the extension program and the marketing of crops. The ex-
perimental coffee program would directly help to increase the quality, pro-
duction and yields of one of Ethiopia's most important export crops. Thus,
while providing important direct benefits to a large segment of the rural
population, the Project would provide a firm basis for the further develop-
ment of the District.



- 21 -
7.02       The Project's internal economic return has been calculated separately
for the four main components: crop development, livestock development, soil
conservation and coffee improvement. Costs and benefits from rural water
supply, assistance to rural artisans and studies were excluded because bene-
fits cannot be quantified. In calculating the rate of return to the economy,
all foreign exchange costs and savings have been valued upward by 33% to re-
flect more adequately the value of foreign exchange to Ethiopia. Farmers
incremental labor has been shadow priced as zero because of lack of alterna-
tive employment opportunities. At this time it cannot be determined to what
extent the recent increase in international grain prices will affect local
prices in Ethiopia. Therefore, 1973 projections for local grain prices were
used, although these are considerably lower than 1974 international price
projections; for this reason 1973 fertilizer prices were used, although these
are also lower than 1974 prices (para. 6.04).
7.03       Under these assumptions the Project's internal economic return
over 20 years would be 17%. (Annex 13) The major risk facing the Project
would be an increase in costs; cost increases of 10% or 20% would reduce the
return to 14% and 11% respectively, while a cost increase of 10% and a benefit
reduction of 10% would reduce it to 10%. The rate of return without shadow
pricing foreign exchange would be 17% for the total Project, 16% for the crop
program, 21% for the livestock program, 11% for the soil conservation program
and 26% for the coffee program.
7.04       Given the current uncertain world market situation, there would
be some risk that the price relationship of fertilizer to grain would change
unfavorably. If this were to happen, farmers participation in the Project
might decrease as only the more efficient farmers could use fertilizer pro-
fitably. However, some costs of WADU's services (80% of which are for
salaries and operating costs) could be reduced in relation to the number
of participating farmers. All capital investments are either related to the
number of staff and can be reduced accordingly or have an independent economic
value. Therefore, a deterioration of the price ratio of fertilizer to grains
is thus unlikely to have a serious adverse effect on the economic return of
the Project.
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
8.01       During negotiations, agreements were reached on the following
principal points:
(a)  Government would assume responsibility for maintenance of
roads and tracks constructed under the Project (para. 4.06);
(b)  Before implementation WADU would submit to IDA the design for
water supply for the two settlements for comments (para 4.07),
its program for assistance to rural artisans for approval
(para 4.08), and the terms of reference and the staffing
arrangements for the three studies to be carried out under the
Project for comments (para 4.21); and



- 22 -
(c) IDA would be consulted prior to appointments or reappointments
to the posts of Director of WADU, Heads of the Extension and
Training, the Marketing and Credit and the Finance Divisions;
and Heads of the Cooperative Development, and the Artificial
Insemination Sections (para 5.03).
8.02       Conditions of Credit effectiveness would be that:
(a) Government would have issued leases to the settlers
established under the first project under terms and
conditions satisfactory to IDA (para 3.07);
(b) A subsidiary loan agreement between Goverrment and AIDB
would have been signed (para 4.24); and
(c) The agreement between AIDB and EPID/WADU which sets the
terms and conditions of EPIDfWADU's responsibilities would
be extended to cover the Project period, and a loan agree-
ment would be concluded between AIDB and WFCU under terms
and conditions satisfactory to IDA (para 5.04).
8.03       The Project is suitable for an IDA Credit of US$12.0 million to
the Empire of Ethiopia.



ANNEX 1
Page 1
ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Crop Production Program
1.        The Project's most important component would be the improvement of
crop production through use of inputs (fertilizer, improved seeds and pesti-
cides). Some additional improvements in crop output would also be expected
from extension services, cooperative development, and other activities supported
by the Project. The increase in production would be determined by the number
of farmers using improved inputs, the area under crops, the cropping patterns
and the expected yield increases caused by the improved inputs.
2.        Farmer Participation.  It is now estimated that at the end of the
first project about 10,000 farmers and 1,750 settlers would have used improved
inputs. By the end of the Project period (year 5) it is expected that 73,000
farmers (49% of all District farmers) would participate, and that about 100,000
farmers (70% of all District farmers) would have been included by year 9.
Extension services would be provided by WADU's Extension, Training and Land
Planning Section. Demonstrators, senior demonstrators and extension agents
would advise on improved agricultural practices and use of improved implements,
and would carry out fertilizer and seed demonstrations, and provide training
for farmer group leaders. Each demonstrator would be expected to supervise
an average of about 500 farmers.
3.        Cropped area, cropping patternsl/1and yield increases are shown in,,
the model farm budgets (Annex 11, Tables 3-7). Estimates of yield increases
are based on first project experience and extensive crop trials carried out
during that period. Estimated cropping patterns are based on a Central
Statistical Office national sample survey and on appraisal findings. An
increase in cultivated area of about 20% (and a corresponding reduction in
fallow land) has been anticipated due to expanded fertilizer use. Farmers
would increase their areas under grains, cotton and chillies accordingly.
4.        Farm Inputs.  Fertilizer would account for about 85% of all farm
inputs. The recommended rate of application would be 1 ql per ha of di-
ammonium phosphate (DAP) on cereals, cotton, ginger and chillies and 2 ql
per ha on irish potatoes. DAP would be supplemented by 0.5 ql per ha urea
on maize, and haricot beans and peas would receive phosphate only. Improved
seed would be made available for cereals, chillies, cotton and pulses. Overall
seed requirements are modest and no difficulty is expected in obtaining
supplies from existing sources. Pesticides would be used on cotton, chillies,
haricot beans, maize and teff. A successful spraying program developed in
settlement areas during the first project would be extended. Cost of seeds,
pesticides and other inputs were estimated at 15% of the total cost of the
Project's fertilizer requirements.
1/   Changes in the cropping pattern have been assumed to take place in year 5.
March 12, 1974

ANNEX 3

ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Rural Road Program
The Project road program would be designed to connect all new
extension and marketing centers with existing district and Imperial Highway
Authority (IHA) roads. It would facilitate supervision of extension, marketing,
livestock (both production and veterinary) and other Project activities and
would improve transportation of inputs and produce within development areas.
Two types of roads would be constructed: minor roads and tracks. In areas of
anticipated higher transport volumes, 366 km of minor roads would be con-
structed; this would include the up-grading to minor road standard of tracks
built under the first project. In order to allow for further penetration of
the development area, 235 km of tracks would also be constructed.
2.        Detailed planning and construction would be the responsibility of
WADU's Infrastructure Section of the Works and Services Division; it would
use machinery owned by WADU (some of which would be provided under the
Project) and labor would be hired directly by WADU. WADU would consult the
local administration and farmers' representatives on the size of land to be
reserved or expropriated by the local administration as well as on alignments
and standards of roads. WADU's Legal Unit would assist the local administration
in clearing property claims.
3.        Maintenance.  No organization exists as yet with overall authority
for road planning, construction and maintenance, although Government is
currently reviewing the results of a study commissioned to determine which
organization should have these responsibilities. Assurances were obtained
that Government would arrange for the maintenance of roads and tracks built
under the Project. Average maintenance costs (10% of construction costs
annually) for roads built under the first and the second Project are estimated
below:
Eth$
1974/75   1975/76   1976/77   1977/78   1978/79   1979/80 onwards
26,000    75,000   118,000   154,000   181,000         206,000
Road Standards
4.        Minor Roads.  These would be dry weather roads passable by light
4-wheel drive vehicles during the rainy season. Minor roads would have a
width of 6 meters, ditch depth of one meter, 14 meters clearing width, a
maximum gradient of 12%, and adequate drainage structures, depending on soil
type. Some stretches would be surfaced with selected local materials.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
Alignment would seek to avoid steep gradients and sharp curves. Average
costs, excluding depreciation and interest on machinery and including WADU's
road construction personnel, all other labor, material and operating cost of
machinery are estimated at Eth$4,200/km.
5.        Tracks.  Two types of tracks would be constructed.  Those in areas
where a rapid increment in transport volume is expected would be built with
all necessary road elements - roadways, shoulders, ditches, and temporary
drainage structures -;for later upgrading to minor road standard by construction
of more permanent drainage structures. They would be passable by light
vehicles in the dry season, and would be used mainly for Project extension and
supervision activities. For the remaining tracks, bush and trees would be
cleared by machinery or labor and no drainage structures would be built;
these would be designed mainly to facilitate non-vehicle transport. Average
costs of all tracks, excluding depreciation and interest on machinery but
including WADU's road construction personnel, all other labor, material and
operating cost of machinery are estimated at Eth$1,200/km.
Phasing of Construction
6.        As indicated below.  Wolamo District has been divided into six
sectors for planning purposes.
Sector I (Northern Sector) located between the IHA road Soddo -
Hossana (now under construction), which it includes and the IHA
road Soddo - Shashemane.
Sector II (Northeast Sector) located between the IHA Soddu -
Shashemane road and the road to Awassa through Bedessa and Bilate
Tena.
Sector III (Southeast Sector) is bounded to the north by the
Soddo - Awassa road which it includes and to the south by the IHA
Soddo - Arba Minch road.
Sector IV (Southern Sector) is bounded to the east by the Soddo -
Arba Minch road which it includes and the west by the Soddo -
Gesupa - Gogora river road (proposed IHA road to Baco).
Sector V (Southwestern Sector) is bounded by the Soddo - Gesupa -
Gogora river road which it includes and the proposed new alignment
of the Soddo Bele - Omo river road.
Sector VI (Northwestern Sector) is bounded to the south by the
new Bele road which it includes and to the east by the Hossana road.
7.        Table 2 indicates the phasing of road construction by sector and year.
May 21, 1974

ANNEX 4
Page 1
ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Rural Water Supply
1.        The Project would provide for construction of rural water facilities
for:
(a) The Abela and Bele settlements established under the first
project (1,750 settlers) and;
(b) About 60,000 smallholder families in the highlands.
2.        Settlements.  Water facilities for the two settlements originally
scheduled for construction under the first project would be included under
the proposed Project. In the Abela settlement a distribution system of about
20 km of lateral pipes along with storage tanks would be added to an existing
pipeline. For the Bele settlement a 9 km pipeline and 20 km of laterals with
storage tanks would constructed. These expanded facilities would reduce the
distances from farms to water points to about 500 m. Design and planning of
the system is expected to be completed under the first project and would be
submitted for IDA comments before implementation. Costs of construction and
maintenance would be recovered by WADU through water charges collected from
settlers.
3.        Highlands.  In order to supply more adequate sources of water for
both men and animals, improve hygiene and prevent erosion caused by cattle
tracks, expanded water facilities would also be provided in the highland
areas. These would supplement similar facilities constructed by the Ministry
of National Community Development, and would draw on experience gained with
demonstration installations completed as part of the first project. The
following types of facilities would be provided:
(a) Spring protection designed to help guard about 87 spring sources
from erosion and pollution provided in conjunction with separate
watering points for human consumption and animals located below
the springs;
(b) Installation of 47 hydrams (hydraulic rams) for lifting river
or spring water onto the ridges;
(c) Construction of 200 communal wells; and
(d) Introduction of roof-catchment of rainwater by use of guttering
and small storage tanks on about 200 individual tin-roofed
houses.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
4.        WADU's Extension, Training and Land Planning Division would select
water sites in consultation with local communities. WADU's water development
staff - one Diploma level technician and two artisans (one plumber, one mason) -
would be responsible for planning and supervision of construction. Local
communities would contribute all unskilled labor and locally available material
free of charge and would contribute at least 25% of cash costs. The District
administration is expected to also contribute 25% of cash costs from revenues
of the district development tax. WADU would contribute 50% of the cash costs
(Eth$140,000) and the services of its general staff directly to the local
communities. Roof-catchments would, however, be paid for through seasonal
credit extended to the home owner.
5.        In year 1, seven of each type of water improvement methods would be
constructed as demonstrations. From year 2 onwards, about 20 facilities for
spring protection, 10 hydrams (3 small and 7 large), and 50 communal wells
would be constructed annually.
6.        The cash cost of construction of a small hydram would be about
Eth$2,750, and that of large ones Eth$5,000. Spring site protection would
cost about Eth$1,000, and well construction about Eth$250. Total cash
costs of rural water facilities for the highlands - inclusive of capital
and staff costs - would be about Eth$140,000 of which about Eth$35,000 would
be subscribed by local communities. All these installations would require,
only simple maintenance, which local communities would be able to supply under
WADU supervision.
7.        Each hydram would provide improved water supply facilities for
about 300 families, each spring site protection for about 500 families and
each well for about 5 families. In total about 60,000 rural families would
benefit from the program.
March 12, 1974

ANNEX 6
