ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Soil Conservation Program
1.        The soil conservation program of the first project was planned to
deal mainly with the two settlements. It achieved its targets there and fur-
thermore initiated the construction of about 350 km of terrace bunds in the
highlands together with some gully stabilitations and introduction of contour
plowing. The program would be extended under the Project to other parts of the
District. In addition, a training program for farmer group leaders would be
introduced. An effort would be made to evaluate the costs and benefits of the
ongoing program, for the lack of such data hampers the formulation and imple-
mentation of larger programs for preserving the already badly eroded arable
land in Ethiopia. The program would encompass three main activities: bund
building and contour cultivation; gully control and land rehabilitation; water
development and program monitoring.
The Program
2.        Bunding and Contour Cultivation.  About 60% of the staff time of
WADU's Land Planning Section would be devoted to this aspect of the program.
Leaders of farmer groups would be trained in line levelling and would train
their members in contour ploughing. It is anticipated that about 4,200 ha/
year (21,000 ha total) would be protected in this way.
3.        Gully Control and Land Rehabilitation.  About 20% of the Land
Planning Section's staff time would be devoted to this element. Gully
control and land rehabilitation would be carried out through a tree planting
program supervised by about 17 demonstrators. Fourteen small tree nurseries
(2 per sub-district of 0.5 ha each) would be established and operated by WADU
to provide tree planting material to farmers free of charge.
4.        Water Development.  The balance of the Land Planning Section's
staff time would be given to water development planning described in Annex 4.
5.        Program Monitoring.  Until data on the costs and benefits of soil
conservation are available, only token conservation components can be added
to development projects. These limited efforts would in no way be adequate
to meet Ethiopia's real and increasing need for effective erosion control
programs. The Project would, therefore, provide for an initial study for
the collection of this data and the design of a more comprehensive system
for extended data collection. The services of consultants or other appro-
priate institutions acceptable to IDA would be retained to:



ANNEX 6
Page 2
(a) Carry out a reconnaissance survey of the Wolamo District to
determine whether a meaningful monitoring program could be
mounted;
(b) If feasible, to design a program including all associated
agronomic trials, soil surveys and structures 1/ required;
Cc) Supervise the construction of measurement and observation
structures and to train Project staff in recording procedures;
(d) Carry out annual supervision over the Project period;
(e) Analyze all data; and
(f) Prepare a report on the results of the program before the end
of Project period.
It is envisaged that the soil conservation study would be similar in nature
to the experiments being conducted by East African Agriculture and Forestry
Research Organization to ascertain the effect of different land uses on
hydrological cycles. Those experiments involve measuring run-off and silt
load from matched catchments (one unimproved as control, the other one
improved by conservation measures outlined above), monitoring of soil condi-
tions and measuring land productivity with agronomic trials. The study would
require about 20 man-months of hydroecologists, agronomists and soil specialists
at a total cost of Eth$200,000.
Benefits
6.        Production from an unprotected land unit with 10-15% slope has been
estimated to decrease 5% in 20 years and 35% in 50 years. Taking into
account a rapid acceleration of deterioration after a slow start, the benefits
of the bunding program (Table 1) would result - if two thirds of WADU's Land
Planning Section's economic costs are deducted - in an economic rate of return
of 11%. Though more spectacular than sheet erosion, gully erosion presents
less of a hazard; hence greater weight has been given to bunding in the con-
servation program. While no attempt has been made to quantify the benefits
of gully control and land rehabiliation, they are also expected to be significant.
I/   Probably flumes to measure run-off incorporating automatic silt samples;
automatic rainfall readers.
March 12, 1974

ANNEX 7
Page 1
ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Livestock Improvement Program
General
1.        The major livestock component included in the Project is a cattle
crossbreeding program for first project areas; the program would be linked to
improved health and extension services, and would aim at raising milk production
and annual offtake from the herd, thus incrasing farmer incomes. The Project
would also include a poultry up-grading schieme.
2.        The crossbreeding program would apply only to the settlements (Zone
D) 1/ and to the two sub-districts covered by the first project (Zone A).
These areas contain about 200,000 head out of the District's total of about
450,000 head of cattle. The 250,000 head in the other areas of the District
(Zones B, C and E) are expected to benefit marginally from increased extension
advice and continuing animal health services.
3.        About 25% of the farmers in Zones A and D with 50,000 head of cattle
are expected to participate in the crossbreeding program; the remaining farmers
in these Zones with 150,000 head would gain from the improved standard of
husbandry and the fodder production and pasture improvement programs carried
out by the expanded livestock extension service. WADU's three livestock
sections (Production, Crossbreeding, and Artificial Insemination and health)
would have responsibility for the program and the latter two sections would
be considerably expanded. Additional investments would be made in the Project
farm to allow for the expanded production of Friesian bulls and improved cocks
for the poultry up-grading scheme.
Crossbreedinig and Artificial Insemination Services
4.        The crossbreeding program established under the first project
provided for the construction of 20 bull stations.  The bulls at these stations
are largely Borana, altnough some are Kenya strain Friesians bred from the
WADU farm herd. Early results from the crossbred Friesian heifers are so
encouraging that the Borana bulls will be phased out.
5.        The Project crossbreeding program would be based on the existing
bull stations, wlhich would be provided with further buildings and equipment
at a cost of Eth$3,500 per station. In light of the successful CADU insenination
program, an artificial insemination service would also be introduced. This
1/   Project Zones are described in Annex 1, Table 1 and in Annex 11.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
would reduce the risk of spreading congenital diseases, simplify the operation,
increase its flexibility and reduce costs. Semen would be supplied initially
by the CADU center which would also train 14 WADU inseminators. The budgeted
cost of semen is Eth$3 per ampule, but in order to encourage farmer participa-
tion, farmers would be charged only Eth$2 per successful insemination - equiva-
lent to the current charge for natural service at bull stations. It is
estimated that of the 20,000 cows crossbred annually, 75% would be inseminated
artificially by year ten - the remainder bulling naturally at the bull stations
as at present. An internationally recruited specialist (for three years), 4
high level staff and 27 demonstrators would carry out the program.
Health Services
6.        During the project the Veterinary Service introduced prophylactic
treatment involving routine inoculation against the more common diseases.
(This supplemented the JP-15 Rinderpest eradication scheme established in
Sidamo Province in 1964 which has been successful; the follow up campaign is
also largely completed.) Vaccines are produced in Ethiopia and inoculation
services are undertaken by a WADU team of 11 vaccinators (2 in most sub-
districts) at demonstrator level, led by two Animal Health Assistants (middle
level staff). They would also now be supported by diagnostic staff. The
WADU vaccinators have been trained by CADU counterparts. An Animal Health
Officer supported by an internationally recruited veterinarian would supervise
the Section. Animal Health Assistants are responsible in specific areas for
monitoring animal health, undertaking inoculations through demonstrators,
disease diagnosis and simple treatment. Farmers have generally responded
well to the program and 40% of the cattle in five of seven sub-districts have
been treated. This service would therefore continue under the Project with
greater diagnosic support services and the assistance of additional extension
demonstrators in first project areas.
Poultry Production
7.        The Project would establish a poultry up-grading scheme involving
the rearing (from day-old chicks to 5 months) of 4,000 improved birds annually
at the WADU Project farm. Because of poultry's prospective important contri-
bution to farmers' incomes and nutritional standards and in view of the desir-
ability for a widespread demonstration effect, farmers would receive mature
cocks on preferential terms, i.e. in exchange for their indigenous cocks.
The anticipated benefits from crossbred birds are increased egg production
(25%), greater meat production (0.5 km per bird) and an improved survival
rate. Farmers are not expected to significantly expand their poultry flocks
and thus the slightly increased feed demands could be satisfied from residues
of farmers' increased crop production.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
Livestock Extension Service
8.        WADU's livestock extension staff would be expanded from 3 to 13.
They would advise farmers participating in the crossbreeding program on live-
stock husbandry, introduction of napier and rhodes grasses; and on supplemen-
tal feeding with sweet potatos, maize and oilseed cake. Napier grass was selec-
tively introduced under the first project and seeds of both grasses have now
been bulked; farmers' response and quantities produced were encouraging.
Napier grass is being planted in sloping fields on the bunds constructed for
soil conservation as a means of improving their stability and farm produc-
tivity. These improved grasses will be generally available, but only Zone
A and D farmers are budgeted to fertilize fodder crops - 2 ql/ha DAP on both
Napier (or Rhodes) grass and sweet potato - producing incremental yields of
60 tons and 9 tons respectively.
Marketing
9.        Cattle.  The main livestock sold in local markets are cattle, as
sheep, goats and poultry are often sold at the farm. Generally, only mature
and cull beasts are sold, with the main buyers being merchants and butchers.
The existing slaughter house is little utilized, mainly because of the high
slaughter fee of Eth$13/head. In order to improve marketing for both buyers
and sellers and to provide additional health facilities, Government recently
implemented the Second Livestock Development Project (Cr 365-ET) with IDA
support. The project involves establishment of 48 primary markets throughout
the country, including stockroutes, seven terminal markets in major consumption
centers, slaughter houses and hides and skins processing sheds. For the Wolamo
District it provides only for the improvement of the one existing slaughter
house and for the erection of four new ones. It makes no provision for stock-
routes or primary market facilities. The nearest proposed stockroute is 40 km
away and the nearest primary market situated at Wendo, 80 km from Soddu. The
nearest terminal market is at Awassa.
10.       Discounting the target increased offtake from the first project
(5,300 cattle per year), the additional offtake from the Project is estimated
at 14,100 head/year, equivalent to approximately 2,500 tons of meat (Table 1).
To ensure adequate market facilities at fair prices and to encourage this
desired offtake the Project would provide for a primary market in Wolamo to
be owned and operated by the Livestock and Meat Board. The Project also
provides for a holding ground of 1,000 ha to be located on marginal land in
the south of the District; WADU would operate this holding ground and the
primary market would be attached. The proposed site - which has already been
allocated to WADU - has natural boundaries and would require little capital
development.
11.       Milk.  The crossbreeding program is expected to increase annual
milk production by 8 million litres by Year 12, which would reach the local
markets mainly as butter. It is assumed that the increased output could be



ANNEX 7
Page 4
handled in the traditional system. If output exceeds local demand, surpluses
could be disposed through the Dairy Development Agency milk production
improvement scheme. This is at present restricted to the vicinity of Addis
Ababa, but a milk collection and processing center is planned for Shashemene,
100 km from Soddu. The Wolamo Farmers Cooperative Union could organize milk
collection and transport.
12.       Poultry Products.  The increase in eggs and poultry meat would
mainly be used for domestic consumption. Surpluses would be small and could
be absorbed by local markets.
Benefits
13.       It is anticipated that participants in the crossbreeding program
would raise the total net value of their livestock production in Year 12 by
Eth$1.0 million and that the net value of the livestock program for the whole
District would be Eth$1.5 million. The program should result in the productioni
of stronger work oxen; in addition the intensive animal health service is ex--
pected to improve milk production surplus to calf requirements of crossbreds
by 650 liters per lactation, the calving rate from 50% to 70% and adult live-
weight by 60% (150 kg). The increased growth rate would advance maturity by
one year and this together with reduced mortality would increase offtake by
51%. Zebu cattle in Zones A and D would benefit from extension and animal
health service; in five years the calving rate would increase from 50% to 52%,
calf mortality would be reduced from 25% to 18%, and cow mortality from 6%
to 4%. Zebu cattle in Zones B, C and E would benefit from more intensive
animal health services which would reduce calf mortality from 25% to 20% and
cow mortality from 6% to 5% over seven years.
Aarch 12, 1974

ANNEX 8

ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Coffee Improvement Program
A. Introduction
1.        Coffee dominates the Ethiopian economy; it contributes about 60%O
of exports, is the source of over half of Government's revenues, and directly
and indirectly provides a livelihood to about a quarter of the population.
Despite its importance, nowever, until recently little effort has been made
to improve either the quantity or quality of the coffee crop. FAO estimates
Ethiopian coffee yields at about 250 kg/ha - half the world average - and this
output is generally of poor quality. Lack, of pruning, weed competition,
incorrect spacing and shade, poor harvesting (stripping rather than selective
picking) and processing all contribute toward these low standards.  There was
little meaningful coffee research in Ethiopia until the late 1960's, and only
marginal progress in attacking these problems has been made thus far. The
IDA supported Coffee Processing Project (Cr 290-ET) provided coffee washing
stations and extension services starting in 1973, but the Project did not
cover Wolamo District. The First Wolamo Project developed recommendations
as to improved yields of smallholder coffee through pruning, stumping1/ and
fertilization. The experimental coffee program would attempt to develop an
extension package which could be carried on initially in Wolamo District, and
ultimately could be extended to all coffee producing areas.
B. The Experimental Coffee Program
2.        The pilot program to be carried out under the Project would involve
1,000 farmers per year, or 5,000 farmers over the Project period. Demonstra-
tors would stump one-seventh of the bushes (about 25 bushes) of participating
farmers. Farmers would also receive extension advice as to retention of suc-
kers, pruning and other cultivation techniques (i.e. weeding, spacing, shade,
mulching and picking) designed to ensure crop quality.  It is anticipated
that farmers would ultimately learn to carry out a multiple-stem pruning
cycle (cutting only one of three stems from same root) whiich has been success-
fully used by smallholders in other East African countries. Should this tech-
nique prove too difficult, a 7-year stumping cycle (all stems from one root
cut at the same time) such as is being investigated at the Jimma Research
Station could be used. In the first project, coffee trials using the multi-
stem pruning technique doubled yields from 300 kg/ha to 600 kg/ha annually.
After stumping, with continued treatment yields would continue to increase
to maximum levels over seven years.
1/   Cutting stems some inches above ground.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
3.        As a result of the stumping program, incomes from the affected
trees would be reduced for a five year period. In order to offset this loss
of income and to attract farmer participation, fertilizer for these trees
would be provided free of charge at a cost of about Eth$5 per farmer over the
program period. In addition a supplemental payment of Eth$10 per farmer
would be made in the year of cutback. The costs of free fertilizer (Eth$5,000)
and the supplementary payment (Eth$79,000) are negligible compared with the
anticipated demonstration effect and economic impact of the program.
C. Benefits
4.        The effect of the program on farmers' income is shown in Table 1.
The cash payment largely offsets reduced sales in the first three years.
From year 6, when the farmer is expected to pay for his fertilizer, his
incremental income from his 25 bushes stumped and fertilized would
be Eth$5.3, rising to Eth$6.6 from year 7 onwards. These figures would apply
to an average of only 0.014 ha or one-seventh of the farmer's coffee area,
but would represent a doubling of his coffee income. It is expected that
this would induce him to improve his entire coffee area, thus achieving an
average incremental income of Eth$46.2 from 0.1 ha of coffee.
5.        The net benefits of the program, are shown in Table 2.  Adjusted
for the economic cost of fertilizer and then discounted, they show an economic
rate of return of 26% 1/. A reduction of value of production by 10% would
reduce the return to 24%, and a reduction by 20% to 22%. If, therefore, a
sound extension package and a widespread demonstration effect results from
this experimental program, it would have a major impact on the Ethiopian
economy. Increased coffee yields could - if exports were not expanded -
lead to a reduction in planted areas, thus allowing other agricultural pro-
duction in these areas.
11   The Table ignores the costs of extension staff and Project overheads;
these would be limited, however, due to the small size of the program
and the fact that the extension staff would operate mainly during the
off-season period and would, therefore, have a low opportunity cost.
March 12, 1974

ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Farm Model Budgets
1.        Five zones have been defined within the Wolamo District in order to
simulate typical farm model budgets without and with development:
Typical Farm
Zone      Designation                Altitude Range (m)          Size (ha)
A     First Project highland         1,700-2,100                  1.3
B     Second Project Ilighland       1,700-2,100                  1.0
C     Second Project highland       Above 2,100                   0.8
D     First Project Settlements    1,300-1,700                    5.0
E     Second Project Lowland         1,300-1,700                  2.5
All highland farmers participating in first project were designated Zone A,
although there are no geographical or ecological differences between Zones A
and B. Zone D represents the settlements established under the first project.
2.        The effect of the Project on individual farms - for uhich a family
size of 4.7 persons has been assumed - is demonstrated in the following ways:
(a) Table 1 compares the benefit-cost ratio of fertilizer and
improved seed for one hectare of different crops;
(b) Table 2 shows the benefit-cost ratio of crop and livestock
improvements (if any) for the zonal farm models for owner-
operators and tenants. All incremental production, whether
sold or consumed by the farm family, has been valued and
farm overhead costs which could not be attributed to different
crops were included;
(c) Tables 3 through 7 show five zonal farm nodel budgets detailed for
crop and livestock production. Here an increase in home-
consumption has been assumed.



ANNEX 11
Page 2
3.        Yields, prices and expected changes in cropping patterns are in line
with those used on calculations of cost and returns of the crop development
program (Annex 1). Farmers in Zone A would improve their crop production
only a little beyond those levels achieved under the first project, while
settlers in Zone D - due to an intensive extension service - would experience
further improved crop production. All settlers and 25% of farmers in Zone A
are expected to participate in the crossbreeding program, the benefits of which
are shown in the model budgets. Livestock in other zones would benefit from
animal health services and improvement of poultry breeding stock. -
4.        Under present conditions, most farms barely feed the families.
Family labor is frequently hired out for additional food or to pay for services
of work oxen leased from other farmer. About half of all farmers are tenants,
who pay their rent partly with a 25% share of their grains, along with cash
and some work on landlords fields (about two days a week).
5.        Livestock is fed on fallow land and crop residues.  For improved
livestock, purchase of concentrates such as neug cake and planting of fertilized
napier grass have been assumed.
March 12, 1974

ANNEX 13
Page 1
ETHIOPIA
SECOND WOLAMO AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis
1.        The Project's internal economic return (IER) is based on separate
calculations for the Crop Development, the Soil Conservation, the Livestock
Improvement and the Coffee Improvement Programs. These calculations exclude
costs and benefits of the following Project components:
(a) Inputs for farmers who participated under the first project
(Zones A and D), and the agricultural extension service for the
settlements (Zone D), as these were included in the first
project's economic calculation;
(b) Rural water supplies for settlements and highland farmers,
assistance to rural artisans, the soil conservation study, the
study on land availability for settlement and the study on
improved ranching regimes, as the benefits of these investments
cannot be quantified.
2.        Costs do not include incremental on-farm labor for production and
soil conservation, nor unskilled labor and locally available material for
cooperative buildings and rural water supply, as the amounts are not signifi-
cant and the opportunity costs of these are assumed to be zero. Benefits
include incremental production of the Project irrespective of whether it would
be sold or used to improve farmers' diets. The other assumptions on which the
economic calculations are based are as follows:
Crop Production Program (Annex 1)
3.   Costs include:
(a) Economic cost of fertilizer at cooperative stores (see Annex 1,
Table 6), including the 15% mark-up charged by cooperatives;
cost of seeds, pesticides and other inputs in the amount of 15%
of fertilizer costs; WADU's contribution to cooperatives (difference
between cooperative revenues from 15% mark-up and total operating
costs (Annex 2, Table 3);
(b) 75% of economic cost of road construction (Anmex 3, Table 1)
and road maintenance. 25% of road benefits are estimated to
accrue to traffic unrelated to project activities;



ANNEX 13
Page 2
(c)  Total economic cost of WADU's services (Annex 5), except
land planning section (60% attributed to soil conservation
program, 40% to rural water supply), the settlement section
(see para 1), Livestock Division, and that part of machinery
and vehicle operating maintenance costs (under workshop division)
which were attributed to other Project activities;
(d) Physical contingencies of 5% on all items.
4.        Benefits.  At this time it cannot be determined towhat extent the
recent increases in international grain prices will affect local prices in
Ethiopia. Therefore 1973 projections for local grain prices (farmgate or
cooperations store, Annex 1 Table 5) were used which are considerably lower
than 1974 projections of international prices, together with 1973 fertilize
prices. As of April 1974 the relation of international prices for grains and
fertilizer has not deteriorated. Benefits were attributed to the project one
year after expenditure on farm inputs to account for the lag between purchases
of inputs and sale of produce.
Livestock Improvement Program (Annex 7)
5.   Costs include:
(a)  Economic cost of fertilizer for napier grass (crossbreeding
program in Zones A and D);
(b)  Economic cost of WADU Livestock Division and corresponding
cost of machinery and vehicle operation and maintenance;
(c) Physical contingencies of 5% on all items.
6.        Benefits.  Only 80% of the benefits of the Livestock Improvement
Program were attributed to the Project, while 20% were assumed to be derived
from first project investments. Prices for cattle and milk products at
local markets have been used (Annex 7, Tables 4, 7, and 9). Benefits from
the poultry improvement scheme have been neglected since they are very small.
Soil Conservation _Program (Annex 6)
7.        Costs include 60% of the economic costs of WADU's Land Planning
Section (Annex 5, Table 4) and related vehicle operating and maintenance
costs for the Project period; and 5% physical contingencies on all items.
S.        Benefits.  Production from an unprotected land unit with a 10% to
15%. slope has been estimated to decrease by 5% in Year 20 and by 35% in
Year 50.  Net value of production saved through erosion control per ha has
been estimated at Eth$150/year. Protection against erosion of 21,000 ha has
been estimated for the Project periocl.



ANNEX 13
Page 3
Coffee Improvement Program (Annex 8)
9.        Costs include the economic cost of fertilizer (Annex 1 Table 6).
The cost of WADU staff has not been included because the Project component is
small and demonstrators would work on coffee in the off-season.
10.       Benefits.  Prices at farm gate or market place of Eth$1.8/kg have
been used for ripe and dry cherries which correspond to 1973 international prices.
Shadow Rate of Foreign Exchange
11.       All foreign exchange costs and savings have been valued upwards by
33% (to a rate of Eth$ 2.75 per US$ 1.00) to reflect more accurately the value
of foreign exchange to the economy of Ethiopia. Project's foreign exchange
savings were estimated at 25% of the value of incremental grain production
on the assumption that they would replace imports.
Internal Economic Return (IER)
12.       With these assumptions, the Project's IER would be 17%.  Without
shadow pricing of foreign exchange, it would also be 17%.
Sensitivity Analysis
13.       Projects major risk would be an increase in costs.  The sensitivity
of the Project's IER to changes in costs and in benefits is shown below:
Crop     Livestock   Soil    Coffee    Total
_______       Development  Improve.  Conser. Improve.  Project
